<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Organism</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Any individual living being or physical living system</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Biological form" redirects here. For the informal taxonomic term in botany, see <a href="/wiki/Race_(biology)#Physiological_race" title="Race (biology)">Race (biology) § Physiological race</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Form of life" redirects here. For the philosophical concept, see <a href="/wiki/Form_of_life_(philosophy)" title="Form of life (philosophy)">Form of life (philosophy)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Living creatures" redirects here. For the biblical concept, see <a href="/wiki/Living_creatures_(Bible)" title="Living creatures (Bible)">Living creatures (Bible)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Fauna and flora" redirects here. For the organization, see <a href="/wiki/Fauna_and_Flora_International" title="Fauna and Flora International">Fauna and Flora International</a>.</div>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amoeba_(Am%C3%B6be)_01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2082" data-file-width="2244" decoding="async" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Amoeba_%28Am%C3%B6be%29_01.jpg/220px-Amoeba_%28Am%C3%B6be%29_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Amoeba_%28Am%C3%B6be%29_01.jpg/330px-Amoeba_%28Am%C3%B6be%29_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Amoeba_%28Am%C3%B6be%29_01.jpg/440px-Amoeba_%28Am%C3%B6be%29_01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amoeba_(Am%C3%B6be)_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a href="/wiki/Amoeba" title="Amoeba">amoeba</a> is a single-celled <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryote</a></div></div></div>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fungi_in_Borneo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="449" decoding="async" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Fungi_in_Borneo.jpg/180px-Fungi_in_Borneo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Fungi_in_Borneo.jpg/270px-Fungi_in_Borneo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Fungi_in_Borneo.jpg/360px-Fungi_in_Borneo.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fungi_in_Borneo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Polypore" title="Polypore">Polypore</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungi" title="Fungi">fungi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flowering_plant" title="Flowering plant">angiosperm</a> trees are large multicellular eukaryotes.</div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>, an <b>organism</b> (from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a> <i> </i><span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%BD%84%CF%81%CE%B3%CE%B1%CE%BD%CE%BF%CE%BD#Ancient_Greek" title="wikt:ὄργανον">ὄργανον</a></span><i> (órganon)</i> 'instrument, implement, tool', and <i> </i><span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/-%CE%B9%CF%83%CE%BC%CF%8C%CF%82#Ancient_Greek" title="wikt:-ισμός">-ισμός</a></span><i> (-ismós)</i>) is any organic, <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">living</a> system that functions as an individual entity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1">[1]</a></sup> All organisms are composed of cells (<a href="/wiki/Cell_theory" title="Cell theory">cell theory</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1">[1]</a></sup> Organisms are classified by <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">taxonomy</a> into groups such as <a href="/wiki/Multicellular_organism" title="Multicellular organism">multicellular</a> <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungi" title="Fungi">fungi</a>; or <a href="/wiki/Unicellular_organism" title="Unicellular organism">unicellular</a> <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Protist" title="Protist">protists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">archaea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> All types of organisms are capable of <a href="/wiki/Reproduction" title="Reproduction">reproduction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Developmental_biology" title="Developmental biology">growth and development</a>, <a href="/wiki/Homeostasis" title="Homeostasis">maintenance</a>, and some degree of response to <a href="/wiki/Stimulus_(physiology)" title="Stimulus (physiology)">stimuli</a>. <a href="/wiki/Beetle" title="Beetle">Beetles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Squid" title="Squid">squids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tetrapod" title="Tetrapod">tetrapods</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mushroom" title="Mushroom">mushrooms</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vascular_plant" title="Vascular plant">vascular plants</a> are examples of multicellular organisms that <a href="/wiki/Cellular_differentiation" title="Cellular differentiation">differentiate</a> specialized <a href="/wiki/Tissue_(biology)" title="Tissue (biology)">tissues</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organ_(anatomy)" title="Organ (anatomy)">organs</a> during <a href="/wiki/Developmental_biology" title="Developmental biology">development</a>.
</p><p>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unicellular" title="Unicellular">unicellular</a> organism may be either a <a href="/wiki/Prokaryote" title="Prokaryote">prokaryote</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryote</a>. Prokaryotes are represented by two separate <a href="/wiki/Three-domain_system" title="Three-domain system">domains</a> – <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">archaea</a>. Eukaryotic organisms are characterized by the presence of a membrane-bound <a href="/wiki/Cell_nucleus" title="Cell nucleus">cell nucleus</a> and contain additional membrane-bound compartments called <a href="/wiki/Organelle" title="Organelle">organelles</a> (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitochondria" title="Mitochondria">mitochondria</a> in animals and plants and <a href="/wiki/Plastid" title="Plastid">plastids</a> in plants and <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a>, all generally considered to be derived from <a href="/wiki/Symbiogenesis" title="Symbiogenesis">endosymbiotic</a> bacteria).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cavaliersmith1987_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-cavaliersmith1987-3">[3]</a></sup> Fungi, animals and plants are examples of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_(biology)" title="Kingdom (biology)">kingdoms</a> of organisms within the eukaryotes.
</p><p>Estimates on the number of Earth's current <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> range from 2 million to 1 trillion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Larsen2017_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Larsen2017-4">[4]</a></sup> of which over 1.7 million have been documented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> More than 99% of all species, amounting to over five billion species,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Book-Biology_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Book-Biology-6">[6]</a></sup> that ever lived are estimated to be <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinct</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StearnsStearns2000_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-StearnsStearns2000-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20141108-MJN_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20141108-MJN-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2016, a set of 355 <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Last_universal_common_ancestor" title="Last universal common ancestor">last universal common ancestor</a> (LUCA) of all organisms from Earth was identified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weissetal_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weissetal-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20160725_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20160725-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The term "organism" (from <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> ὀργανισμός, <i>organismos</i>, from ὄργανον, <i>organon</i>, i.e. "instrument, implement, tool, organ of sense or apprehension")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LSJ_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-LSJ-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OnlineEtDict_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-OnlineEtDict-12">[12]</a></sup> first appeared in the English language in 1703 and took on its current definition by 1834 (<a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">Oxford English Dictionary</a>). It is directly related to the term "organization". There is a long tradition of defining organisms as self-organizing beings, going back at least to <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Immanuel Kant</a>'s 1790 <i><a href="/wiki/Critique_of_Judgment" title="Critique of Judgment">Critique of Judgment</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions">Definitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Definitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>An organism may be defined as an assembly of <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecules</a> functioning as a more or less stable whole that exhibits the <a href="/wiki/Life#Biology" title="Life">properties of life</a>. Dictionary definitions can be broad, using phrases such as "any living structure, such as a plant, animal, fungus or bacterium, capable of growth and reproduction".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chambers_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chambers-14">[14]</a></sup> Many definitions exclude <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a> and possible man-made <a href="/wiki/Hypothetical_types_of_biochemistry" title="Hypothetical types of biochemistry">non-organic life</a> forms, as viruses are dependent on the biochemical machinery of a host cell for reproduction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-OED-15">[15]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Superorganism" title="Superorganism">superorganism</a> is an organism consisting of many individuals working together as a single functional or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_unit" title="Social unit">social unit</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>There has been controversy about the best way to define the organism<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> and indeed about whether or not such a definition is necessary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Several contributions<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> are responses to the suggestion that the category of "organism" may well not be adequate in biology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (November 2016)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Viruses">Viruses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Viruses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Non-cellular_life" title="Non-cellular life">Non-cellular life</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">Viruses</a> are not typically considered to be organisms because they are incapable of autonomous <a href="/wiki/Reproduction" title="Reproduction">reproduction</a>, growth or <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a>. Although some organisms are also incapable of independent survival and live as obligatory intracellular <a href="/wiki/Parasitism" title="Parasitism">parasites</a>, they are capable of independent metabolism and procreation. Although viruses have a few <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> and molecules characteristic of living organisms, they have no metabolism of their own; they cannot synthesize and organize the organic compounds from which they are formed. Naturally, this rules out autonomous reproduction: they can only be passively replicated by the machinery of the <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">host cell</a>. In this sense, they are similar to inanimate matter.
</p><p>While viruses sustain no independent metabolism and thus are usually not classified as organisms, they do have their own <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a>, and they do <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolve</a> by mechanisms similar to the evolutionary mechanisms of organisms. Thus, an argument that viruses should be classed as living organisms is their ability to undergo evolution and replicate through self-assembly. However, some scientists argue that viruses neither evolve nor self-reproduce. Instead, viruses are evolved by their host cells, meaning that there was co-evolution of viruses and host cells. If host cells did not exist, viral evolution would be impossible. This is not true for cells. If viruses did not exist, the direction of cellular evolution could be different, but cells would nevertheless be able to evolve. As for reproduction, viruses totally rely on hosts' machinery to replicate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10reasons_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-10reasons-31">[31]</a></sup> The discovery of viruses with genes coding for energy metabolism and protein synthesis fuelled the debate about whether viruses are living organisms. The presence of these genes suggested that viruses were once able to metabolize. However, it was found later that the genes coding for energy and protein metabolism have a cellular origin. Most likely, these genes were acquired through <a href="/wiki/Horizontal_gene_transfer" title="Horizontal gene transfer">horizontal gene transfer</a> from viral hosts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10reasons_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-10reasons-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry">Chemistry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Chemistry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Organisms are complex chemical systems, organized in ways that promote reproduction and some measure of sustainability or survival. The same laws that govern non-living chemistry govern <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">the chemical processes of life</a>. It is generally the phenomena of entire organisms that determine their fitness to an environment and therefore the survival of their <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a>-based genes.
</p><p>Organisms clearly owe their origin, metabolism, and many other internal functions to chemical phenomena, especially the chemistry of large organic molecules. Organisms are complex systems of <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">chemical compounds</a> that, through interaction and environment, play a wide variety of roles.
</p><p>Organisms are semi-closed chemical systems. Although they are individual units of life (as the definition requires), they are not closed to the environment around them. To operate they constantly take in and release energy. <a href="/wiki/Autotroph" title="Autotroph">Autotrophs</a> produce usable energy (in the form of organic compounds) using light from the sun or inorganic compounds while <a href="/wiki/Heterotroph" title="Heterotroph">heterotrophs</a> take in organic compounds from the environment.
</p><p>The primary <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> in these compounds is <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Chemical_property" title="Chemical property">chemical properties</a> of this element such as its great affinity for bonding with other small atoms, including other carbon atoms, and its small size making it capable of forming multiple bonds, make it ideal as the basis of organic life. It is able to form small three-atom compounds (such as <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>), as well as large chains of many thousands of atoms that can store data (<a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid" title="Nucleic acid">nucleic acids</a>), hold cells together, and transmit information (protein).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Macromolecules">Macromolecules</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Macromolecules">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Compounds that make up organisms may be divided into <a href="/wiki/Macromolecule" title="Macromolecule">macromolecules</a> and other, smaller molecules. The four groups of macromolecule are <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid" title="Nucleic acid">nucleic acids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipids</a>. Nucleic acids (specifically deoxyribonucleic acid, or DNA) store genetic data as a sequence of <a href="/wiki/Nucleotide" title="Nucleotide">nucleotides</a>. The particular sequence of the four different types of nucleotides (<a href="/wiki/Adenine" title="Adenine">adenine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cytosine" title="Cytosine">cytosine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guanine" title="Guanine">guanine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Thymine" title="Thymine">thymine</a>) dictate many characteristics that constitute the organism. The sequence is divided up into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Codon" title="Codon">codons</a>, each of which is a particular sequence of three nucleotides and corresponds to a particular <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acid</a>. Thus a sequence of DNA codes for a particular protein that, due to the chemical properties of the amino acids it is made from, <a href="/wiki/Protein_folding" title="Protein folding">folds</a> in a particular manner and so performs a particular function.
</p><p>These protein functions have been recognized:
</p>
<ol><li><a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">Enzymes</a>, which catalyze the reactions of metabolism</li>
<li>Structural proteins, such as <a href="/wiki/Tubulin" title="Tubulin">tubulin</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Collagen" title="Collagen">collagen</a></li>
<li>Regulatory proteins, such as <a href="/wiki/Transcription_factor" title="Transcription factor">transcription factors</a> or cyclins that regulate the cell cycle</li>
<li>Signaling molecules or their receptors such as some <a href="/wiki/Hormone" title="Hormone">hormones</a> and their receptors</li>
<li>Defensive proteins, which can include everything from <a href="/wiki/Antibody" title="Antibody">antibodies</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">immune system</a>, to toxins (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Dendrotoxin" title="Dendrotoxin">dendrotoxins</a> of snakes), to proteins that include unusual amino acids like <a href="/wiki/Canavanine" title="Canavanine">canavanine</a></li></ol>
<p>A bilayer of <a href="/wiki/Phospholipid" title="Phospholipid">phospholipids</a> makes up the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phospholipid_membrane" title="Phospholipid membrane">membrane</a> of cells that constitutes a barrier, containing everything within a cell and preventing compounds from freely passing into, and out of, the cell. Due to the selective permeability of the phospholipid membrane, only specific compounds can pass through it.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure">Structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>All organisms consist of structural units called <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cells</a>; some contain a single cell (unicellular) and others contain many units (multicellular). Multicellular organisms are able to specialize cells to perform specific functions. A group of such cells is a <a href="/wiki/Tissue_(biology)" title="Tissue (biology)">tissue</a>, and in animals these occur as four basic types, namely <a href="/wiki/Epithelium" title="Epithelium">epithelium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nervous_tissue" title="Nervous tissue">nervous tissue</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muscle_tissue" title="Muscle tissue">muscle tissue</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Connective_tissue" title="Connective tissue">connective tissue</a>. Several types of tissue work together in the form of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organ_(anatomy)" title="Organ (anatomy)">organ</a> to produce a particular function (such as the pumping of the blood by the <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">heart</a>, or as a barrier to the environment as the <a href="/wiki/Skin" title="Skin">skin</a>). This pattern continues to a higher level with several organs functioning as an <a href="/wiki/Organ_system" title="Organ system">organ system</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_system" title="Reproductive system">reproductive system</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digestive_system" title="Digestive system">digestive system</a>. Many multicellular organisms consist of several organ systems, which coordinate to allow for life.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cell">Cell</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Cell">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Cell_theory" title="Cell theory">cell theory</a>, first developed in 1839 by <a href="/wiki/Matthias_Jakob_Schleiden" title="Matthias Jakob Schleiden">Schleiden</a> and <a href="/wiki/Theodor_Schwann" title="Theodor Schwann">Schwann</a>, states that all organisms are composed of one or more cells; all cells come from preexisting cells, and cells contain the <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">hereditary information</a> necessary for regulating cell functions and for transmitting information to the next generation of cells.
</p><p>There are two types of cells, eukaryotic and prokaryotic. Prokaryotic cells are usually singletons, while eukaryotic cells are usually found in multicellular organisms. Prokaryotic cells lack a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_membrane" title="Nuclear membrane">nuclear membrane</a> so <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> is unbound within the cell; eukaryotic cells have nuclear membranes.
</p><p>All cells, whether prokaryotic or eukaryotic, have a <a href="/wiki/Cell_membrane" title="Cell membrane">membrane</a>, which envelops the cell, separates its interior from its environment, regulates what moves in and out, and maintains the <a href="/wiki/Membrane_potential" title="Membrane potential">electric potential of the cell</a>. Inside the membrane, a <a href="/wiki/Salt" title="Salt">salty</a> <a href="/wiki/Cytoplasm" title="Cytoplasm">cytoplasm</a> takes up most of the cell volume. All cells possess DNA, the hereditary material of <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a>, containing the information necessary to <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">build</a> various <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a>, the cell's primary machinery. There are also other kinds of <a href="/wiki/Biomolecule" title="Biomolecule">biomolecules</a> in cells.
</p><p>All cells share several similar characteristics of:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AlbertsCh1_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-AlbertsCh1-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Reproduction by <a href="/wiki/Cell_division" title="Cell division">cell division</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Binary_fission" title="Binary fission">binary fission</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mitosis" title="Mitosis">mitosis</a> or <a href="/wiki/Meiosis" title="Meiosis">meiosis</a>).</li>
<li>Use of enzymes and other proteins <a href="/wiki/Genetic_code" title="Genetic code">coded</a> by DNA genes and made via <a href="/wiki/Messenger_RNA" title="Messenger RNA">messenger RNA</a> intermediates and <a href="/wiki/Ribosome" title="Ribosome">ribosomes</a>.</li>
<li>Metabolism, including taking in raw materials, building cell components, converting energy, <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecules</a> and releasing <a href="/wiki/By-product" title="By-product">by-products</a>. The functioning of a cell depends upon its ability to extract and use chemical energy stored in organic molecules. This energy is derived from <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_pathway" title="Metabolic pathway">metabolic pathways</a>.</li>
<li>Response to external and internal <a href="/wiki/Signal_transduction" title="Signal transduction">stimuli</a> such as changes in temperature, <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> or nutrient levels.</li>
<li>Cell contents are contained within a <a href="/wiki/Cell_membrane" title="Cell membrane">cell surface membrane</a> that contains proteins and a <a href="/wiki/Lipid_bilayer" title="Lipid bilayer">lipid bilayer</a>.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolutionary_history">Evolutionary history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Evolutionary history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Origin_of_life" title="Origin of life">Origin of life</a>, <a href="/wiki/Earliest_known_life_forms" title="Earliest known life forms">Earliest known life forms</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Common_descent" title="Common descent">Common descent</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Last_universal_common_ancestor">Last universal common ancestor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Last universal common ancestor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stromatolites.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="371" data-file-width="550" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Stromatolites.jpg/220px-Stromatolites.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Stromatolites.jpg/330px-Stromatolites.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Stromatolites.jpg/440px-Stromatolites.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stromatolites.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Precambrian" title="Precambrian">Precambrian</a> <a href="/wiki/Stromatolite" title="Stromatolite">stromatolites</a> in the Siyeh Formation, <a href="/wiki/Glacier_National_Park_(U.S.)" title="Glacier National Park (U.S.)">Glacier National Park</a>. In 2002, a paper in the scientific journal <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">Nature</a></i> suggested that these 3.5 <a href="/wiki/Billion_years" title="Billion years">Gya</a> (billion years old) <a href="/wiki/Geological_formation" title="Geological formation">geological formations</a> contain fossilized <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a> microbes. This suggests they are evidence of one of the earliest known life forms on Earth.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Last_universal_common_ancestor" title="Last universal common ancestor">Last universal common ancestor</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_evolutionary_history_of_life" title="Timeline of the evolutionary history of life">Timeline of the evolutionary history of life</a></div>
<p>The last universal common ancestor (LUCA) is the most recent organism from which all organisms now living on Earth <a href="/wiki/Common_descent" title="Common descent">descend</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theobald_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-theobald-33">[33]</a></sup> Thus it is the <a href="/wiki/Most_recent_common_ancestor" title="Most recent common ancestor">most recent common ancestor</a> of all current life on Earth. The LUCA is estimated to have lived some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timeline_of_evolution" title="Timeline of evolution">3.5 to 3.8 billion years ago</a> (sometime in the <a href="/wiki/Paleoarchean" title="Paleoarchean">Paleoarchean</a> <a href="/wiki/Era" title="Era">era</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> The earliest evidence for <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life on Earth</a> is <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">graphite</a> found to be <a href="/wiki/Biogenic_substance" title="Biogenic substance">biogenic</a> in 3.7 billion-year-old <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metasediment" title="Metasediment">metasedimentary rocks</a> discovered in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Greenland" title="Western Greenland">Western Greenland</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NG-20131208_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-NG-20131208-36">[36]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Microbial_mat" title="Microbial mat">microbial mat</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossils" title="Fossils">fossils</a> found in 3.48 billion-year-old <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">sandstone</a> discovered in <a href="/wiki/Western_Australia" title="Western Australia">Western Australia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP-20131113_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-AP-20131113-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AST-20131108_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-AST-20131108-38">[38]</a></sup> Although more than 99 percent of all species that ever lived on the planet are estimated to be extinct,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StearnsStearns2000_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-StearnsStearns2000-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20141108-MJN_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20141108-MJN-8">[8]</a></sup> it is likely that more than a billion species of life exist on Earth currently, with the highest estimates and projections reaching one trillion species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Larsen2017_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Larsen2017-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Information about the early development of life includes input from many different fields, including <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">geology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Planetary_science" title="Planetary science">planetary science</a>. These sciences provide information about the history of the Earth and the changes produced by life. However, a great deal of information about the early Earth has been destroyed by geological processes over the course of <a href="/wiki/Time" title="Time">time</a>.
</p><p>All organisms are descended from a common ancestor or ancestral gene pool. Evidence for common descent may be found in traits shared between all living organisms. In Darwin's day, the evidence of shared traits was based solely on visible observation of morphologic similarities, such as the fact that all birds have wings, even those that do not fly.
</p><p>There is strong evidence from genetics that all organisms have a common ancestor. For example, every living cell makes use of <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid" title="Nucleic acid">nucleic acids</a> as its genetic material, and uses the same twenty <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a> as the building blocks for <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>. All organisms use the same <a href="/wiki/Genetic_code" title="Genetic code">genetic code</a> (with some extremely rare and minor deviations) to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Translation_(genetics)" title="Translation (genetics)">translate</a> nucleic acid sequences into proteins. The universality of these traits strongly suggests common ancestry, because the selection of many of these traits seems arbitrary. <a href="/wiki/Horizontal_gene_transfer" title="Horizontal gene transfer">Horizontal gene transfer</a> makes it more difficult to study the last universal ancestor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> However, the universal use of the same genetic code, same nucleotides, and same amino acids makes the existence of such an ancestor overwhelmingly likely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theo_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-theo-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phylogeny">Phylogeny</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Phylogeny">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r984819063">.mw-parser-output table.clade{border-spacing:0;margin:0;font-size:100%;line-height:100%;border-collapse:separate;width:auto}.mw-parser-output table.clade table.clade{width:100%;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-label{width:0.7em;padding:0 0.15em;vertical-align:bottom;text-align:center;border-left:1px solid;border-bottom:1px solid;white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-fixed-width{overflow:hidden;text-overflow:ellipsis}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-fixed-width:hover{overflow:visible}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-label.first{border-left:none;border-right:none}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-label.reverse{border-left:none;border-right:1px solid}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-slabel{padding:0 0.15em;vertical-align:top;text-align:center;border-left:1px solid;white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-slabel:hover{overflow:visible}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-slabel.last{border-left:none;border-right:none}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-slabel.reverse{border-left:none;border-right:1px solid}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-bar{vertical-align:middle;text-align:left;padding:0 0.5em;position:relative}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-bar.reverse{text-align:right;position:relative}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-leaf{border:0;padding:0;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-leafR{border:0;padding:0;text-align:right}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-leaf.reverse{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output table.clade:hover span.linkA{background-color:yellow}.mw-parser-output table.clade:hover span.linkB{background-color:green}</style>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Location_of_the_root">Location of the root</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Location of the root">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Reduktiver_Acetyl-CoA-Weg.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3007" data-file-width="3648" decoding="async" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Reduktiver_Acetyl-CoA-Weg.png/300px-Reduktiver_Acetyl-CoA-Weg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Reduktiver_Acetyl-CoA-Weg.png/450px-Reduktiver_Acetyl-CoA-Weg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Reduktiver_Acetyl-CoA-Weg.png/600px-Reduktiver_Acetyl-CoA-Weg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Reduktiver_Acetyl-CoA-Weg.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>LUCA may have used the <a href="/wiki/Wood%E2%80%93Ljungdahl_pathway" title="Wood–Ljungdahl pathway">Wood–Ljungdahl or reductive acetyl–CoA pathway</a> to fix carbon.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For branching of Bacteria phyla, see <a href="/wiki/Bacterial_phyla" title="Bacterial phyla">Bacterial phyla</a>.</div>
<p>The most commonly accepted location of the root of the <a href="/wiki/Tree_of_life_(biology)" title="Tree of life (biology)">tree of life</a> is between a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monophyletic" title="Monophyletic">monophyletic</a> <a href="/wiki/Domain_(biology)" title="Domain (biology)">domain</a> <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">Bacteria</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clade</a> formed by <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">Archaea</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eukaryota" title="Eukaryota">Eukaryota</a> of what is referred to as the "traditional tree of life" based on several molecular studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> A very small minority of studies have concluded differently, namely that the root is in the domain Bacteria, either in the phylum <a href="/wiki/Bacillota" title="Bacillota">Bacillota</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> or that the phylum <a href="/wiki/Chloroflexota" title="Chloroflexota">Chloroflexota</a> is basal to a clade with Archaea and Eukaryotes and the rest of Bacteria as proposed by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cavalier-Smith" title="Thomas Cavalier-Smith">Thomas Cavalier-Smith</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CS2_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-CS2-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>Research published in 2016, by <a href="/wiki/William_F._Martin" title="William F. Martin">William F. Martin</a>, by genetically analyzing 6.1 million protein-coding genes from sequenced prokaryotic genomes of various phylogenetic trees, identified 355 protein clusters from amongst 286,514 protein clusters that were probably common to the LUCA. The results "depict LUCA as <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_organism" title="Anaerobic organism">anaerobic</a>, CO<sub>2</sub>-fixing, H<sub>2</sub>-dependent with a <a href="/wiki/Wood%E2%80%93Ljungdahl_pathway" title="Wood–Ljungdahl pathway">Wood–Ljungdahl pathway</a> (the reductive <a href="/wiki/Acetyl-CoA" title="Acetyl-CoA">acetyl-coenzyme A</a> pathway), N<sub>2</sub>-fixing and thermophilic. LUCA's biochemistry was replete with FeS clusters and radical reaction mechanisms. Its cofactors reveal dependence upon <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transition_metals" title="Transition metals">transition metals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flavin_mononucleotide" title="Flavin mononucleotide">flavins</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S-adenosyl_methionine" title="S-adenosyl methionine">S-adenosyl methionine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coenzyme_A" title="Coenzyme A">coenzyme A</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ferredoxin" title="Ferredoxin">ferredoxin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Molybdopterin" title="Molybdopterin">molybdopterin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Corrin" title="Corrin">corrins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Selenium" title="Selenium">selenium</a>. Its genetic code required <a href="/wiki/Nucleoside" title="Nucleoside">nucleoside</a> modifications and S-adenosylmethionine-dependent <a href="/wiki/Methylation" title="Methylation">methylations</a>." The results depict <a href="/wiki/Methanogen" title="Methanogen">methanogenic</a> <a href="/wiki/Clostridium" title="Clostridium">clostria</a> as a basal clade in the 355 lineages examined, and suggest that the LUCA inhabited an anaerobic <a href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_vent" title="Hydrothermal vent">hydrothermal vent</a> setting in a geochemically active environment rich in H<sub>2</sub>, CO<sub>2</sub>, and iron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weissetal_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Weissetal-9">[9]</a></sup> However, the identification of these genes as being present in LUCA was criticized, suggesting that many of the proteins assumed to be present in LUCA represent later horizontal gene transfers between archaea and bacteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproduction">Reproduction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Reproduction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Reproduction" title="Reproduction">Reproduction</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sexual_reproduction" title="Sexual reproduction">Sexual reproduction</a> is widespread among current eukaryotes, and was likely present in the last common ancestor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> This is suggested by the finding of a core set of genes for meiosis in the descendants of lineages that diverged early from the eukaryotic evolutionary tree.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> and Malik et al.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> It is further supported by evidence that eukaryotes previously regarded as "ancient asexuals", such as <i><a href="/wiki/Amoeba" title="Amoeba">Amoeba</a></i>, were likely sexual in the past, and that most present day asexual amoeboid lineages likely arose recently and independently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>In prokaryotes, <a href="/wiki/Transformation_(genetics)#Natural_transformation" title="Transformation (genetics)">natural bacterial transformation</a> involves the transfer of DNA from one bacterium to another and integration of the donor DNA into the recipient chromosome by recombination. Natural bacterial transformation is considered to be a primitive sexual process and occurs in both bacteria and archaea, although it has been studied mainly in bacteria. Transformation is clearly a bacterial adaptation and not an accidental occurrence, because it depends on numerous gene products that specifically interact with each other to enter a state of <a href="/wiki/Natural_competence" title="Natural competence">natural competence</a> to perform this complex process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Transformation is a common mode of DNA transfer among prokaryotes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Horizontal_gene_transfer">Horizontal gene transfer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Horizontal gene transfer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Horizontal_gene_transfer" title="Horizontal gene transfer">Horizontal gene transfer</a></div>
<p>The ancestry of living organisms has traditionally been reconstructed from morphology, but is increasingly supplemented with phylogenetics – the reconstruction of phylogenies by the comparison of genetic (DNA) sequence.
</p>
<blockquote><p>Sequence comparisons suggest recent <a href="/wiki/Horizontal_gene_transfer" title="Horizontal gene transfer">horizontal transfer</a> of many genes among diverse <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> including across the boundaries of <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetics" title="Phylogenetics">phylogenetic</a> "domains". Thus determining the phylogenetic history of a species can not be done conclusively by determining evolutionary trees for single genes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Biologist Peter Gogarten suggests "the original metaphor of a tree no longer fits the data from recent genome research", therefore "biologists (should) use the metaphor of a mosaic to describe the different histories combined in individual genomes and use (the) metaphor of a net to visualize the rich exchange and cooperative effects of HGT among microbes."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Future_of_life_.28cloning_and_synthetic_organisms.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Future_of_life_(cloning_and_synthetic_organisms)">Future of life (cloning and synthetic organisms)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Organism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Future of life (cloning and synthetic organisms)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Modern biotechnology is challenging traditional concepts of organisms and species. <a href="/wiki/Cloning" title="Cloning">Cloning</a> is the process of creating a new multicellular organism, genetically identical to another, with the potential of creating entirely new species of organisms. Cloning is the subject of <a href="/wiki/Ethics_of_cloning" title="Ethics of cloning">much ethical debate</a>.
</p><p>In 2008, the <a href="/wiki/J._Craig_Venter_Institute" title="J. Craig Venter Institute">J. Craig Venter Institute</a> assembled a synthetic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bacterial_genome_size" title="Bacterial genome size">bacterial genome</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Mycoplasma_genitalium" title="Mycoplasma genitalium">Mycoplasma genitalium</a></i>, by using recombination in yeast of 25 overlapping DNA fragments in a single step. The use of yeast recombination greatly simplifies the assembly of large DNA molecules from both synthetic and natural fragments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Venter_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Venter-58">[58]</a></sup> Other companies, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synthetic_Genomics" title="Synthetic Genomics">Synthetic Genomics</a>, have already been formed to take advantage of the many commercial uses of custom designed genomes.
</p>

<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.species2000.org/" rel="nofollow">Species 2000 Indexing the world's known species</a>. Species 2000 has the objective of enumerating all known species of plants, animals, fungi and microbes on Earth as the baseline dataset for studies of global biodiversity. It will also provide a simple access point enabling users to link from here to other data systems for all groups of organisms, using direct species-links.</li>
